Appendices

## APPENDIX 8

How to hatch brine shrimp (Artemia salina) cystsHOW TO HATCH BRINE SHRIMP (ARTEMIA SALINA) CYSTS

(Source: Brine Shrimp Direct)

Introduction and storage

The hatchlings of brine shrimp (Artemia salina) cysts are excellent feed for catfish swim

up-fry. They are called naupplii and are of the same growth stage as catfish yolk sac-fry.

Adult shrimps in nature can produce live nauplii immediately, but in adverse circumstances

immediately, but in adverse circumstances they produce capsulated cysts. When the situation gets better the cysts will hatch, even after a period of many months. The cysts float on the water and can be scooped, water and can be scoped, cleaned,

dried and canned for aquaculture purposes.

Purchased packaged brine shrimp cysts should always be kept under dark, dry and cool conditions. When the cysts are stored for months, it is better to put them in a freezer! Brine shrimp cysts, which are used immediately or in the upcoming weeks, should be separated

from the main stock

Hatching of Artemia cysts

Salt and pH

Artemia cysts start hatching when they are put in a saline solution of 25 g/l; the water

should be aerated so that the cysts remain in suspension. Preferably, the salt used salt used for the incubation should be sea salt or industrial salt. Don't use salt which is processed and enriched with iodine and chemicals which avoid clumping of the salt. If the pH of the solution is 8 or below, it should be elevated to a level above 8 with sodium bicarbonate

(NaHCO(NaHCO $ _{3/3} $)..

## Light

Illumination is necessary to trigger the hatching mechanism within the embryo during the

first few hours of incubation. For an optimal hatching rate, it is advisable to leave the light

on for the entire incubation period.

Temperature

hours.

The incubation time is depending on the water temperature; 26-28 $ ^{\circ} \mathrm{C} $ is optimal. At this temperature hatching of Artemia cysts will take about 24

## Aeration

234

Appendices

Aeration is necessary to keep the cysts in suspension and to keep at least 3 mg/l oxygen in the water.

Stocking density of the cysts

The best stocking density for the cysts is 1 g/l. Higher stocking densities result in a lower hatching rate.

Containers

Funnel shaped containers are best for hatching Artemia cysts (Photo 13A).

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_151.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>

Photo 13A. A locally made incubator for Artemia cysts.

Harvest and feeding

from their shells. After several minutes the shells will float on the surface while the nauplii

will be at the bottom. The bottom of the container has a valve with a fine meshed sieve

(see Photo 31, chapter 8). Harvesting can begin now.

Rinse the harvested nauplii with fresh water before feeding them to the catfish swim

up-fry, because the hatching medium is dirty and contains a lot of bacteria. The best way

forward is to harvest the nauplii directly after hatching, because the quality of freshly

hatched Artemia cysts is higher. Surplus nauplii can be frozen and fed as ice cubes.

Fresh Artemia nauplii stay alive in fresh water for hours, so the catfish fry will have a lot of

time to consume them.

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_152.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>



Glossary

238

Aerobic process: a biological process which requires oxygen.

Amino acids: the basic units of proteins, which are the building blocks of any living creature. The sequence of the amino acids in the protein determines the type

of protein. There are only 23 different amino acids. Some of them are essential in

a diet in order to make it complete.

Anaerobic process: a biological process which takes place in the absence of

oxygen. Antifungal additives: additives that inhibit the growth of fungus, which

important to increase the shelf life of products like fish feed. During the rainy

season especially, when humidity is high and the environment is favourable for fungus.

Arborescent organ: this organ absorbs oxygen directly from the atmosphere. It consists of cauliflower-like structures situated on the gills supported by cartilage

and covered by highly vascularised tissue.

Artemia salina: a species of brine shrimp which can be found in salt lakes all over the world. The shrimps produce eggs which are encapsulated and float on the water. These cysts are harvested, dried, cleaned and sold to fish hatcheries with the purpose of hatching them in salt water and feeding these newly hatched eggs

(nauplii) to the fish larval stages.

Binary fission: a reproduction system of singled-cell organisms by one cell splitting into two new cells which creates two new organisms.

Biofiltration: a system which enables degradation of pollutants by bacteria in a

bioreactor.

Biomass: at the fish farm this is the mass of living fish.

at the farm against harmful bacteria, parasites, viruses or biochemical substances. Branchial arches: gill arches with a bony structure which support the gills. Break-even point: the point where total cost (expenses) and total sales (revenue)

are the same.

Biosecurity: procedures and/or measures to protect the fish population

Broad spectrum antibiotic: an antibiotic that acts against a wide range of disease-causing bacteria.

Blood agar: a with blood enriched bacterial growth medium. Some bacteria such as streptococci don't grow well if blood is not added to the medium.

Cash flow: payments that bring money into and take money out of a business.

Chorion: the outermost membrane surrounding the embryo.

Caudal fin: the tail fin of a fish.

Cryopreservation: the use of freezing at very low temperatures to preserve structurally intact living cells such as sperm.

Coliforms: rod shaped Gram-negative bacteria. They are a commonly used indicator for the sanitary quality of food and water.

Denitrification: a microbiological process which reduces nitrate to molecular

nitrogen under anaerobic conditions.

Digestible fraction: the fraction of feed that can be broken down in the stomach and the intestines, and subsequentially be absorbed by the body.

Dorsal fin: the unpaired fin on the back of a fish which stabilises it against rolling and assists it in sudden turns.

Ectotherm: an animal that is dependent on external sources for body heat.

Ectoparasites: parasites that live on the outside of their host.

Endotherm: an animal that generates its own body heat.

Endoparasites: parasites that live inside their host-animal.

Essential nutritional factors: vital compounds that the body can't make by itself,

and are therefore essential for food to prevent diseases and ensure growth and

Extruder: a machine by which a set of mixed ingredients is forced through an opening in a perforated plate or die. In fish feed milling, the pellet produced in an extruder expands when it leaves the die and causes the feed to float, since air has

been incorporated into the pellet.

Facultative anaerobic organism: a single-celled organism which can live with or without oxygen.

FCR for fish: feed conversion rate for fish which is expressed as the amount of feed needed to grow 1 kg of fish.

Fisheries: businesses or industries devoted to catching fish and the science of

producing fish for the purpose of providing human food.

Gaussian distribution: the continuous probability distribution shown in a chart. Normally a bell-shaped curve. A higher curve implies that your efforts towards your target are being increasingly met.

Gill filaments: the red, fleshy part of the gills. These filaments absorb the oxygen

Gill rakers: bony structures for filtering particles or food out of the water. They don't have a respiratory function.

Gonadotropin releasing hormone: this hormone is responsible for releasing

other hormones from the pituitary, such as the follicle stimulating hormone which

induces the ovulation of the eggs.

Gram-negative bacteria: these bacteria will not retain the crystal violet stain

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_153.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>

Growth curve of fish: an S-shaped curve which represents the growth pattern of fish and other living creatures. The growth starts slowly, then accelerates into an exponential growth rate, followed by a decline until almost zero.

Growth rate expression: the growth rate of a living creature is always expressed as

a % of a unit in a period of time. For the growth rate of fish, it's expressed as a % of the bodyweight in a day. For example, if the fish grow 5% a day, it implies that a fish of 100 g grows 5 g in a day.

GSI: the gonadosomatic index (tes or ovary). The weight of the gonads is divided by

the total tissue weight x 100%. For example, a female fish of 5 kg has 500 g of eggs.

Haemoglobin in fish: a protein in the red blood cells that transports oxygen from the gills to the rest of the body.

The GSI of the female fish is: 500/5000 x 100% = 10%.

Hammer mill: in a fish feed plant this is a machine used for crushing raw materials,

such as maize and soya, into powder by repeated blows of small hammers.

Harmattan: a season on the West African subcontinent, from early December until mid-March, with a dry, dusty north-east wind which originates from the Sahara Desert.

Hypoxia: not enough oxygen in the tissues to sustain normal body functions.

Invertebrates: animals that lack a vertebral column or backbone.

Locomotory: ability to move from one place to another.

Maintenance feeding level in fish: the level of feeding which allows the fish neither to grow nor to lose weight.

Maxillary barbels: a single pair of catfish barbels that point sideways from the corners of the mouth. They are the longest barbels of the catfish.

Mandibular barbels: two pairs of catfish barbels pointing downwards.

Metabolic fraction: the fraction of the feed which is responsible for growth.

Micronutrients: the essential vitamins and minerals in the feed that fish cannot

produce themselves.

Micropyle: a small opening in the fish egg, through which sperm cells can enter.

MSS-222: tricaine mesylate is a sedation chemical for catfish which consists of a white

powder. At a dosage of 2 g/10 l of water, the catfish will be sedated after a few

minutes.

NAFDAC: National Agency for food and drug administration and control.

Nasal barbels: a single pair of catfish barbels pointing upwards.

Nitrification: the biological oxidation of ammonia to nitrite followed by oxidation of

nitrite to nitrate.

Non-specific defence system of fish: the organs of a fish which protect it against every invader in the same way. This system includes physical barriers, such as the skin and the slime, and the white blood cells which attack any strange invader coming into

the body.

Non-specific disease signs: signs which are not specific to a particular disease but are common for many different diseases.

Olfactory organ: the fish's organ for sense of smell.

Operculum: the gill cover.

Pectoral fins: a single pair of fins situated just behind the gills on each side.

PHCN: Power Holding Company Nigeria (before NEPA).

Pituitary gland: a gland that is embedded in the brain and regulates many physiological processes, such as reproduction.

RAS: recirculating aquaculture system.

Respiration: an aerobic or anaerobic chemical reaction in living cells which releases energy.

Salmonids: a fish family widely farmed in cold and moderate climates. Trout and salmon are the most common species within this family.

Self-feeder: a feeding system which allows the fish to eat when they want to,

because they can initiate the feed distribution themselves.

Septicaemia: blood poisoning, which occurs when a bacterial infection in one part of the body is spread to other parts in the body via the bloodstream causing multiple infections.

Specific defence system of fish: antibodies that are produced by lymphocytes

in response to a hostile invasion. They are specific to the invader or antigen and inactivate it. With a second invasion of the same antigen, the fish's defence system is already prepared and the disease will not break out. Thanks to the antibodies the fish has become immune to this disease.

SOP: standard operating procedure.

Standing stock: at the fish farm this is the quantity or mass of fish which is responsible for the continuous growth of fish to the selling size.

Stocking density: the number of fish in a certain area, usually expressed as number of fish per m².

SWOT analysis: a technique for understanding strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats of a business idea or plan.

TAN: total ammonia nitrogen, which is the total amount of nitrogen in water in the forms of NH3 and NH4+.

Trematode: a fluke that has the ability to attach itself to a host with anchors.

Urbanisation: the shift of a population from farm areas to town areas.

Urogenital: related to both the urinary and genital organs.

Ventral fins: a pair of fins which starts after the pectoral fins before the urogenital system.

Vitellogenesis: the production of yolk in the egg.

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_154.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>

## WORD OF THANKS

Word of thanks

A word of thanks to all the people who have helped me realize this book. In particular, I want to mention Mr. Steve Elias of S.C.C. Ltd., who motivated me to start writing this book. I also want to thank my friends Adama Enyojo and Iyodo Dominic for helping with all the illustrations in the book, making it practical and easy to read, and my wife Mercy for encouraging me to complete this huge task.

Last but not least, I have a special word of thanks for Heleen van der Maas for her important editing role without changing the context. Certainly she underestimated the work, but never complained. Heleen, it was a real pleasure cooperating with you.

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_155.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>

'African catfish farmer's handbook' is a practical handbook written by Willy Fleuren from a background in aquaculture and a wealth of experience in the practical management of cultured fisheries and in different production systems. It is filled with interesting learning points and nuggets of wisdom for a successful enterprise in fish farming. In addition, the author presents his points in the simplest, most understandable, and adaptive form.

<div style='text-align: center;'><img src='images/img_156.png' alt='OCR图片'/></div>

Willy Fleuren (58) has been involved in fish farming in Nigeria since 1998. He pioneered the introduction and establishment of the first Recirculation Aquaculture System in Nigeria. A believer in technology transfer, he trained the first set of Nigerians on its use, and they worked directly with him on the early prototypes of the system. This led to an initial explosive wide acceptance of the system through the train-the-trainer approach.

My interest in aquaculture and fisheries is why I took the time to read the manual. It is a handbook that anyone can enjoy. It will be useful for students, start-up and established entrepreneurs, researchers, input suppliers, colleges, universities and government officials. I highly recommend this interesting and educational handbook to all persons interested in fisheries and aquaculture. It is an excellent guide and must have reference material.

Born in the Netherlands, where Willy Fleuren started fish farming to complete his MSC degree in Aquaculture in 1984, he has been living in Nigeria since 2009. Presently Mr Fleuren lives in Abuja with his wife and children.

Foluke O Areola,

Former Federal Director (Fisheries, Fisheries & Aquaculture, Quarantine)

First Female & Past National President, Fisheries Society of Nigeria (FISON).
